Asymptotic notation convention
We adopt the following asymptotic notation. Each line below has the same meaning
for positive functions рқ‘“ and рқ‘” as some parameter, usually рқ‘ӣ, tends to infinity:
вҖў рқ‘“ вүІ рқ‘”, рқ‘“ = рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘”), рқ‘” = О©( рқ‘“ ), рқ‘“ вүӨ рқҗ¶рқ‘” (for some constant рқҗ¶ > 0)
вҖў рқ‘“ /рқ‘” вҶ’ 0, рқ‘“ вүӘ рқ‘”, рқ‘“ = рқ‘ң(рқ‘”) (and sometimes рқ‘” = рқң”( рқ‘“ ))
вҖў рқ‘“ = Оҳ(рқ‘”), рқ‘“ вүҚ рқ‘”, рқ‘” вүІ рқ‘“ вүІ рқ‘”
вҖў рқ‘“ вҲј рқ‘”, рқ‘“ = (1 + рқ‘ң(1))рқ‘”
вҖў whp (= with high probability) means with probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1)
Warning. Analytic number theorists use вүӘ to mean рқ‘Ӯ (В·) (Vinogradov notation),
differently from how the symbol is used in these notes.
Subscripts (e.g., рқ‘Ӯ рқ‘  ( ), вүІрқ‘  ) are used to emphasize that the hidden constants may depend
on the subscripted parameters. For example, рқ‘“ (рқ‘ , рқ‘Ҙ) вүІрқ‘  рқ‘”(рқ‘ , рқ‘Ҙ) means that for every рқ‘ 
there is some constant рқҗ¶рқ‘  so that рқ‘“ (рқ‘ , рқ‘Ҙ) вүӨ рқҗ¶рқ‘  рқ‘”(рқ‘ , рқ‘Ҙ) for all рқ‘Ҙ.
We write [рқ‘Ғ] := {1, . . . , рқ‘Ғ }.

1 Introduction
The probabilistic method is an important technique in combinatorics. In a typical
application, we wish to prove the existence of something with certain desirable properties. To do so, we devise a random construction and show that it works with positive
probability.
Let us begin with a simple example of this method.
Theorem 1.0.1 (Large bipartite subgraph)

Every graph with рқ‘ҡ edges has a bipartite subgraph with at least рқ‘ҡ/2 edges.
Proof. Let the graph by рқҗә = (рқ‘ү, рқҗё). Assign every vertex a color, randomly either

black or white, uniformly and independently at random.
Let рқҗё вҖІ be the set of edges with one black endpoint and one white endpoint. Then
(рқ‘ү, рқҗё вҖІ) is a bipartite subgraph of рқҗә.
Every edge belongs to рқҗё вҖІ with probability 1/2. So by the linearity of expectation, the
expected size of рқҗё вҖІ is
1
E[|рқҗё вҖІ |] = |рқҗё | .
2
Thus there is some coloring with |рқҗё вҖІ | вүҘ 12 |рқҗё |. Then (рқ‘ү, рқҗё вҖІ) is the desired subgraph.
в–Ў

1.1 Lower bounds to Ramsey numbers
Ramsey number рқ‘№(рқ’Ң, в„“) = smallest рқ‘ӣ such that in every red-blue edge coloring of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ ,
there exists a red рқҗҫ рқ‘ҳ or a blue рқҗҫв„“ .
For example, рқ‘…(3, 3) = 6 (every red/blue edge-coloring of рқҗҫ6 has a monochromatic
triangle, but one can color рқҗҫ5 without any monochromatic triangle).
Ramsey (1929) proved that рқ‘…(рқ‘ҳ, в„“) exists (i.e., is finite). This is known as RamseyвҖҷs
theorem.

1

1 Introduction

Paul ErdЕ‘s (1913вҖ“1996) is considered the father of the probabilistic method.
He published around 1,500 papers during his lifetime, and had more than 500
collaborators. To learn more about ErdЕ‘s, see his biography The man who loved
only numbers by Hoffman and the documentary N is a number (You may be able
to watch this movie for free on Kanopy using your local public library account).

Frank Ramsey (1903вҖ“1930) wrote seminal papers in philosophy, economics, and
mathematical logic, before his untimely death at the age of 26 from liver problems.
See a recent profile of him in the New Yorker.

Finding quantitative estimates of Ramsey numbers (and its generalizations) is generally
a difficult and often fundamental problem in Ramsey theory.
Remark 1.1.1 (Hungarian names). Many Hungarian mathematicians, notable due to

ErdЕ‘sвҖҷ influence, made foundational contributions to this field. So we will encounter
many Hungarian names in this subject.

вҖңErdГ¶sвҖқ)

ErdЕ‘sвҖҷ original proof

ErdЕ‘s in his seminal paper:
P. ErdЕ‘s, Some remarks on the theory of graphs, Bull. Amer. Math. Soc, 1947.

Remark 1.1.4 (Constructive lower bounds). The above proof only gives the existence

of a red-blue edge-coloring of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ without monochromatic cliques. Is there a way to
find algorithmically find one? With an appropriate рқ‘ӣ, even though a random coloring
achieves the goal with very high probability, there is no efficient method (in polynomial
running time) to certify that any specific edge-coloring avoids monochromatic cliques.

3

1 Introduction
So even though there are lots of Ramsey colorings, it is hard to find and certify an
actual one. This difficulty has been described as finding hay in a haystack.
Finding constructive lower bounds is a major open problem. There was major progress
on this problem stemming from connections to randomness extractors in computer
science (e.g., Barak et al. 2012, Chattopadhyay & Zuckerman 2016, Cohen 2017)
Remark 1.1.5 (Ramsey number upper bounds). Although Ramsey proved that Ram-

sey numbers are finite, his upper bounds are quite large. ErdЕ‘sвҖ“Szekeres (1935) used
a simple and nice inductive argument to show

Recently, in a major and surprising breakthrough, Campos, Griffiths, Morris, and
Sahasrabudhe (2023+) show that there is some constant рқ‘җ > 0 so that for all sufficiently
large рқ‘ҳ,

This is the first exponential improvement over the ErdЕ‘sвҖ“Szekeres bound.

Alteration method
Let us give another argument that slightly improves the earlier lower bound on Ramsey
numbers.
Instead of just taking a random coloring and analyzing it, we first randomly color, and
then fix some undesirable features. This is called the alteration method (sometimes
also the deletion method).
Theorem 1.1.6 (Ramsey lower bound via alteration)

 
рқ‘ӣ 1вҲ’ ( рқ‘ҳ)
For any рқ‘ҳ, рқ‘ӣ, we have рқ‘…(рқ‘ҳ, рқ‘ҳ) > рқ‘ӣ вҲ’
2 2 .
рқ‘ҳ
Proof. We construct an edge-coloring of a clique in two steps:

(1) Randomly color each edge of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ with red or blue (independently and uniformly
at random);
(2) Delete a vertex from every monochromatic рқҗҫ рқ‘ҳ .
The process yields a 2-edge-colored clique with no monochromatic рқҗҫ рқ‘ҳ (since the
second step destroyed all monochromatic cliques).

LovГЎsz local lemma
Often we wish to avoid a set of вҖңbad eventsвҖқ рқҗё 1 , . . . , рқҗё рқ‘ӣ . Here are two easy extremes:
ГҚ
вҖў (Union bound) If рқ‘– P(рқҗёрқ‘– ) < 1, then union bound tells us that we can avoid all
bad events.
вҖў (Independence) If all bad events are independent, then the probability that none
ГҺрқ‘ӣ
of рқҗёрқ‘– occurs is рқ‘–=1
(1 вҲ’ P(рқҗёрқ‘– )) > 0 (provided that all P(рқҗёрқ‘– ) < 1).
What if we are in some intermediate situation, where the union bound is not good
enough, and the bad events are not independent, but there are only few dependencies?
The LovГЎsz local lemma provides us a solution when each event is only independent
with all but a small number of other events.
Here is a version of the LovГЎsz local lemma, which we will prove later in Chapter 6.

5

1 Introduction

let рқҗёрқ‘– be an event that depends only on the variables indexed by рқҗөрқ‘– (i.e., рқҗёрқ‘– is allowed
to depend only on {рқ‘Ҙ рқ‘— : рқ‘— вҲҲ рқҗөрқ‘– }).
Suppose, for every рқ‘– вҲҲ [рқ‘ҡ], рқҗөрқ‘– has non-empty intersections with at most рқ‘‘ other рқҗө рқ‘— вҖҷs,

Here рқ‘’ = 2.71 В· В· В· is the base of the natural logarithm. This constant turns out to be
optimal in the above theorem.

1.2 Set systems
In extremal set theory, we often wish to understand the maximum size of a set system
with some given property. A set system F is a collection of subsets of some ground
set, usually [рқ‘ӣ]. That is, each element of F is a subset of [рқ‘ӣ]. We will see some classic
results from extremal set theory each with a clever probabilistic proof.

SpernerвҖҷs theorem
We say that a set family F is an antichain if no element of F is a subset of another
element of F (i.e., the elements of F are pairwise incomparable by containment).
Question 1.2.1

What is the maximum number of sets in an antichain of subsets of [рқ‘ӣ]?


The set F = [рқ‘ӣ]
all рқ‘ҳ-element subsets of [рқ‘ӣ]) has size рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ . It is an antichain
рқ‘ҳ (i.e.,


(why?). The size рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ is maximized when рқ‘ҳ = рқ‘ӣ2 or рқ‘ӣ2 . The next result shows that
this is indeed the best we can do.

BollobГЎsвҖҷ two families theorem
Theorem 1.2.4 (BollobГЎsвҖҷ two families theorem 1965)

Let рқҗҙ1 , . . . , рқҗҙрқ‘ҡ be рқ‘ҹ-element sets and рқҗө1 , . . . , рқҗөрқ‘ҡ be рқ‘ -element sets such that рқҗҙрқ‘– вҲ© рқҗөрқ‘– =

вҲ… for all рқ‘– and рқҗҙрқ‘– вҲ© рқҗө рқ‘— вү  вҲ… for all рқ‘– вү  рқ‘—. Then рқ‘ҡ вүӨ рқ‘ҹ+рқ‘ 
рқ‘ҹ .
This bound is sharp: let рқҗҙрқ‘– range over all рқ‘ҹ-element subsets of [рқ‘ҹ + рқ‘ ] and set рқҗөрқ‘– =
[рқ‘ҹ + рқ‘ ] \ рқҗҙрқ‘– .
Let us give an application/motivation for BollobГЎsвҖҷ two families theorem in terms of
transversals. Given a set family F , say that рқ‘Ү is a transversal for F if рқ‘Ү вҲ© рқ‘Ҷ вү  вҲ… for
all рқ‘Ҷ вҲҲ F (i.e., рқ‘Ү hits every element of F ). Let рққү(F), the transversal number of F ,
be the size of the smallest transversal of F . Say that F is рққү-critical if рқңҸ(F вҖІ) < рқңҸ(F )
whenever F вҖІ is a proper subset of F .

рқ‘ҹ .

BollobasвҖҷ two families theorem has many interesting generalizations that we will not

proofs of this theorem and its extensions.

ErdЕ‘sвҖ“KoвҖ“Rado theorem on intersecting families
We say that a family F is intersecting if рқҗҙ вҲ© рқҗө вү  вҲ… for all рқҗҙ, рқҗө вҲҲ F . That is, no two
sets in F are disjoint.
Here is an easy warm up.
Question 1.2.7 (Intersecting familyвҖ”unrestricted sizes)

What is the largest intersecting family of subsets of [рқ‘ӣ]?
One way to generate a large intersecting family is to include all sets that contain a fixed
element (say, the element 1). This family has size 2рқ‘ӣвҲ’1 and is clearly intersecting.
(This isnвҖҷt the only example with size 2рқ‘ӣвҲ’1 ; can you think of other intersecting families
with the same size?)

9

1 Introduction
It turns out that one cannot do better than 2рқ‘ӣвҲ’1 . Since we can pair up each subset of [рқ‘ӣ]
with its complement. At most one of рқҗҙ and [рқ‘ӣ] \ рқҗҙ can be in an intersecting family.
And so at most half of all sets can be in an intersecting family.
The question becomes much more interesting if we restrict to рқ‘ҳ-uniform families.
Question 1.2.8 ( рқ‘ҳ -uniform intersecting family)

What is the largest intersecting family of рқ‘ҳ-element subsets of [рқ‘ӣ]?
Example: F = all subsets containing the element 1. Then F is intersecting and

|F | = рқ‘ӣвҲ’1
рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 .
Theorem 1.2.9 (ErdЕ‘sвҖ“KoвҖ“Rado 1961; proved in 1938)

If рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 2рқ‘ҳ, then every intersecting family of рқ‘ҳ-element subsets of [рқ‘ӣ] has size at most
рқ‘ӣвҲ’1 
рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 .
Remark 1.2.10. The assumption рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 2рқ‘ҳ is necessary since if рқ‘ӣ < 2рқ‘ҳ, then the family

of all рқ‘ҳ-element subsets of [рқ‘ӣ] is automatically intersecting by pigeonhole.
Proof. Consider a uniform random circular permutation of 1, 2, . . . , рқ‘ӣ (arrange them

randomly around a circle)
For each рқ‘ҳ-element subset рқҗҙ of [рқ‘ӣ], we say that рқҗҙ is contiguous if all the elements of
рқҗҙ lie in a contiguous block on the circle.

The probability that рқҗҙ forms a contiguous set on the circle is exactly рқ‘ӣ/ рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ .

So the expected number of contiguous sets in F is exactly рқ‘ӣ |F | / рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ .
Since F is intersecting, there are at most рқ‘ҳ contiguous sets in F (under every circular
ordering of [рқ‘ӣ]). Indeed, suppose that рқҗҙ вҲҲ F is contiguous. Then there are 2(рқ‘ҳ вҲ’ 1)
other contingous sets (not necessarily in F ) that intersect рқҗҙ, but they can be paired
off into disjoint pairs (check! Here we use the hypothesis that рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 2рқ‘ҳ). Since F is
intersecting, it follows that it contains at most рқ‘ҳ contiguous sets.

Combining with result from the previous paragraph, we see that рқ‘ӣ |F | / рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ вүӨ рқ‘ҳ, and


hence |F | вүӨ рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ рқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ = рқ‘ӣвҲ’1

1.3 2-colorable hypergraphs
An рқ’Ң-uniform hypergraph (or рқ’Ң-graph) is a pair рқҗ» = (рқ‘ү, рқҗё), where рқ‘ү (vertices)

is a finite set and рқҗё (edges) is a set of рқ‘ҳ-element subsets of рқҗё, i.e., рқҗё вҠҶ рқ‘үрқ‘ҳ . (So
hypergraphs and set families are the same concept, just different names.)

10

1.3 2-colorable hypergraphs
We say that рқҗ» is рқ’“-colorable if the vertices can be colored using рқ‘ҹ colors so that no
edge is monochromatic.
Let рқ’Һ(рқ’Ң) denote the minimum number of edges in a рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph that is
not 2-colorable (elsewhere in the literature, вҖң2-colorableвҖқ = вҖңproperty BвҖқ, named after
Bernstein who introduced the concept in 1908). Some small values:
вҖў рқ‘ҡ(2) = 3
вҖў рқ‘ҡ(3) = 7. Example: Fano plane (below) is not 2-colorable (the fact there are no
6-edge non-2-colorable 3-graphs can be proved by exhaustive search).

вҖў рқ‘ҡ(4) = 23, proved via exhaustive computer search (Г–stergГҘrd 2014)
Exact value of рқ‘ҡ(рқ‘ҳ) is unknown for all рқ‘ҳ вүҘ 5. However, we can get some asymptotic
lower and upper bounds using the probability method.
Theorem 1.3.1 (ErdЕ‘s 1964)

рқ‘ҡ(рқ‘ҳ) вүҘ 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 for every рқ‘ҳ вүҘ 2.
(In other words, every рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph with fewer than 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 edges is 2-colorable.)
Proof. Let there be рқ‘ҡ < 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 edges. In a random 2-coloring, the probability that there

is a monochromatic edge is вүӨ 2вҲ’рқ‘ҳ+1 рқ‘ҡ < 1.

в–Ў

Remark 1.3.2. Later in Section 3.5 we will prove an better lower bound рқ‘ҡ(рқ‘ҳ) вүі

вҲҡпёҒ
2 рқ‘ҳ рқ‘ҳ/log рқ‘ҳ, which is the best known to date.
Perhaps somewhat surprisingly, the state of the art upper bound is also proved using
probabilistic method (random construction).
Theorem 1.3.3 (ErdЕ‘s 1964)

рқ‘ҡ(рқ‘ҳ) = рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘ҳ 2 2 рқ‘ҳ ).
(In other words, there exists a рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph with рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘ҳ 2 2 рқ‘ҳ ) edges that is not
2-colorable.)
Proof. Let |рқ‘ү | = рқ‘ӣ = рқ‘ҳ 2 (this choice is motivated by the displayed inequality be-

low). Let рқҗ» be the рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph obtained by choosing рқ‘ҡ edges рқ‘Ҷ1 , . . . , рқ‘Ҷ рқ‘ҡ

1.4 List chromatic number of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ
Given a graph рқҗә, its chromatic number рққҢ(рқ‘®) is the minimum number of colors
required to proper color its vertices.
In list coloring, each vertex of рқҗә is assigned a list of allowable colors. We say that рқҗә
is рқ’Ң-choosable (also called рқ’Ң-list colorable) if it has a proper coloring no matter how
one assigns a list of рқ‘ҳ colors to each vertex.
We write ch(рқ‘®), called the list chromatic number (also called: choice number,
choosability, list colorability) of рқҗә, to be the smallest рқ‘ҳ so that рқҗә is рқ‘ҳ-choosable.
We have рқң’(рқҗә) вүӨ ch(рқҗә) by assigning the same list of colors to each vertex. The
inequality may be strict, as we will see below.
For example, while every bipartite graph is 2-colorable, рқҗҫ3,3 is not 2-choosable.
Indeed, no list coloring of рқҗҫ3,3 is possible with color lists (check!):

1.4 List chromatic number of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ
{1, 2}

{1, 2}

{1, 3}

{1, 3}

{2, 3}

{2, 3}

Exercise: check that ch(рқҗҫ3,3 ) = 3.
Question 1.4.1

What is the asymptotic behavior of ch(рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ )?
First we prove an upper bound on ch(рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ ).
Theorem 1.4.2

If рқ‘ӣ < 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 , then рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ is рқ‘ҳ-choosable.


In other words, ch(рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ ) вүӨ log2 (2рқ‘ӣ) + 1.
Proof. For each color, mark it either L or R independently and uniformly at random.

For any vertex of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ on the left part, remove all its colors marked R.
For any vertex of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ on the right part, remove all its colors marked L.
The probability that some vertex has no colors remaining is at most 2рқ‘ӣ2вҲ’рқ‘ҳ < 1 by the
union bound. So with positive probability, every vertex has some color remaining.
Assign the colors arbitrarily for a valid coloring.
в–Ў
The lower bound on ch(рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ ) turns out to follow from the existence of non-2-colorable
рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph with many edges.
Theorem 1.4.3

If there exists a non-2-colorable рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph with рқ‘ӣ edges, then рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ is not
рқ‘ҳ-choosable.
Proof. Let рқҗ» = (рқ‘ү, рқҗё) be a non-2-colorable рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph |рқҗё | = рқ‘ӣ edges.

Now, view рқ‘ү as colors and assign to the рқ‘–-th vertex of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ on both the left and right
bipartitions a list of colors given by the рқ‘–-th edge of рқҗ». We leave it as an exercise to
check that this рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ is not list colorable.
в–Ў
Recall from Theorem 1.3.3 that there exists a non-2-colorable рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph
with рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘ҳ 2 2 рқ‘ҳ ) edges. Thus ch(рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ ) > (1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1)) log2 рқ‘ӣ.
Putting these bounds together:

13

1 Introduction

Corollary 1.4.4 (List chromatic number of a complete bipartite graph)

ch(рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ ) = (1 + рқ‘ң(1)) log2 рқ‘ӣ
It turns out that, unlike the chromatic number, the list chromatic number always
grows with the average degree. The following result was proved using the method of
hypergraph containers, a very important modern development in combinatorics that
we will see a glimpse of in Chapter 11. It provides the optimal asymptotic dependence
(the example of рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ shows optimality).
Theorem 1.4.5 (Saxton and Thomason 2015)

If a graph рқҗә has average degree рқ‘‘, then, as рқ‘‘ вҶ’ вҲһ,
ch(рқҗә) > (1 + рқ‘ң(1)) log2 рқ‘‘.
They also proved similar results for the list chromatic number of hypergraphs. For
graphs, a slightly weaker result, off by a factor of 2, was proved earlier by Alon (2000).

does not contain рқ‘ҳ + 1 elements forming a chain: рқҗҙ1 вҠҠ В· В· В· вҠҠ рқҗҙ рқ‘ҳ+1 . Prove that
F is no larger than taking the union of the рқ‘ҳ levels of the Boolean lattice closest
to the middle layer.
6. Let рқҗә be a graph on рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 10 vertices. Suppose that adding any new edge to рқҗә
would create a new clique on 10 vertices. Prove that рқҗә has at least 8рқ‘ӣ вҲ’ 36 edges.
Hint: Apply BollobГЎsвҖҷ two families theorem

all four colors are represented.

F | рқҗҙ contains all 2 рқ‘ҳ subsets of рқҗҙ.
9. Let рқҗҙ1 , . . . , рқҗҙрқ‘ҡ be рқ‘ҹ-element sets and рқҗө1 , . . . , рқҗөрқ‘ҡ be рқ‘ -element sets. Suppose

2 Linearity of Expectations
Linearity of expectations refers to the following basic fact about the expectation: given
random variables рқ‘Ӣ1 , . . . , рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ and constants рқ‘җ 1 , . . . , рқ‘җ рқ‘ӣ ,
E[рқ‘җ 1 рқ‘Ӣ1 + В· В· В· + рқ‘җ рқ‘ӣ рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ ] = рқ‘җ 1 E[рқ‘Ӣ1 ] + В· В· В· + рқ‘җ рқ‘ӣ E[рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ ].
This identity does not require any assumption of independence. On the other hand,
generally E[рқ‘Ӣрқ‘Ң ] вү  E[рқ‘Ӣ]E[рқ‘Ң ] unless рқ‘Ӣ and рқ‘Ң are uncorrelated (independent random
variables are always uncorrelated).
Here is a simple application (there are also much more involved solutions via enumeration methods).
Question 2.0.1 (Expected number of fixed points)

What is the average number of fixed points of a uniform random permutation of an рқ‘ӣ
element set?
Solution. Let рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– be the event that element рқ‘– вҲҲ [рқ‘ӣ] is fixed. Then E[рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– ] = 1/рқ‘ӣ. The

expected number of fixed points is
E[рқ‘Ӣ1 + В· В· В· + рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ ] = E[рқ‘Ӣ1 ] + В· В· В· + E[рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ ] = 1.

в–Ў

2.1 Hamiltonian paths in tournaments
We frequently use the following fact:
with positive probability, рқ‘Ӣ вүҘ E[рқ‘Ӣ] (likewise for рқ‘Ӣ вүӨ E[рқ‘Ӣ]).
A tournament is a directed complete graph. A Hamilton path in a directed graph is a
directed path that contains every vertex exactly once.
Question 2.1.1 (Number of Hamilton paths in a tournament)

What is the maximum (and minimum) number of Hamilton paths in an рқ‘ӣ-vertex
tournament?

17

2 Linearity of Expectations
The minimization problem is easier. The transitive tournament (i.e., respecting a fixed
linear ordering of vertices) has exactly one Hamilton path. On the other hand, every
tournament has at least one Hamilton path (Exercise: prove this! Hint: consider a
longest directed path).
The maximization problem is more difficult and interesting. Here we have some
asymptotic results.
Theorem 2.1.2 (Tournaments wit many Hamilton paths; Szele 1943)

There is a tournament on рқ‘ӣ vertices with at least рқ‘ӣ!2вҲ’(рқ‘ӣвҲ’1) Hamilton paths
Proof. Consider a random tournament where every edge is given a random orientation

chosen uniformly and independently. Each of the рқ‘ӣ! permutations of vertices forms a
directed path with probability 2вҲ’рқ‘ӣ+1 . So that expected number of Hamilton paths is
рқ‘ӣ!2вҲ’рқ‘ӣ+1 . Thus, there exists a tournament with at least this many Hamilton paths.
в–Ў
This was considered the first use of the probabilistic method. Szele conjectured that the
maximum number of Hamilton paths in a tournament on рқ‘ӣ players is рқ‘ӣ!/(2 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1)) рқ‘ӣ .
This was proved by Alon (1990) using the MincвҖ“BrГ©gman theorem on permanents (we
will see this later in Chapter 10 on the entropy method).

2.2 Sum-free subset
A subset рқҗҙ in an abelian group is sum-free if there do not exist рқ‘Һ, рқ‘Ҹ, рқ‘җ вҲҲ рқҗҙ with
рқ‘Һ + рқ‘Ҹ = рқ‘җ.
Does every рқ‘ӣ-element set contain a large sum-free set?
Theorem 2.2.1 (Large sum-free subsets; ErdЕ‘s 1965)

Every set of рқ‘ӣ nonzero integers contains a sum-free subset of size вүҘ рқ‘ӣ/3.
Proof. Let рқҗҙ вҠҶ Z \ {0} with | рқҗҙ| = рқ‘ӣ. For рқңғ вҲҲ [0, 1], let

рқҗҙрқңғ := {рқ‘Һ вҲҲ рқҗҙ : {рқ‘Һрқңғ} вҲҲ (1/3, 2/3)}
where {В·} denotes fractional part. Then рқҗҙрқңғ is sum-free since (1/3, 2/3) is sum-free in
R/Z.
For рқңғ uniformly chosen at random, {рқ‘Һрқңғ} is also uniformly random in [0, 1], so P(рқ‘Һ вҲҲ
рқҗҙрқңғ ) = 1/3. By linearity of expectations, E| рқҗҙрқңғ | = рқ‘ӣ/3.
в–Ў

18

2.3 TurГЎnвҖҷs theorem and independent sets
Remark 2.2.2 (Additional results). Alon and Kleitman (1990) noted that one can

improve the bound to вүҘ (рқ‘ӣ + 1)/3 by noting that | рқҗҙрқңғ | = 0 for рқңғ close to zero (say,
|рқңғ| < (3 max рқ‘Һ вҲҲ рқҗҙ |рқ‘Һ|) вҲ’1 ), so that | рқҗҙрқңғ | < рқ‘ӣ/3 with positive probability, and hence
| рқҗҙрқңғ | > рқ‘ӣ/3 with positive probability. Note that since | рқҗҙрқңғ | is an integer, being > рқ‘ӣ/3 is
the same as being вүҘ (рқ‘ӣ + 1)/3.
Bourgain (1997) improved it to вүҘ (рқ‘ӣ + 2)/3 via a difficult Fourier analytic argument.
This is currently the best lower bound known.
It remains an open problem to prove вүҘ (рқ‘ӣ + рқ‘“ (рқ‘ӣ))/3 for some function рқ‘“ (рқ‘ӣ) вҶ’ вҲһ.
In the other direction, Eberhard, Green, and Manners (2014) showed that there exist
рқ‘ӣ-element sets of integers whose largest sum-free subset has size вүӨ (1/3 + рқ‘ң(1))рқ‘ӣ.

Question 2.3.1 (TurГЎn problem)

Taking the complement of a graph changes its independent sets to cliques and vice
versa. So the problem is equivalent to one about graphs without large independent
sets.
The following result, due to Caro (1979) and Wei (1981), shows that a graph with
small degrees much contain large independent sets. The probabilistic method proof
shown here is due to Alon and Spencer.

Remark 2.3.4 (Derandomization). Here is an alternative вҖңgreedy algorithmвҖқ proof

of the CaroвҖ“Wei inequality. At each step, take a vertex of smallest degree, and remove
it and all its neighbors. If each vertex рқ‘Ј is assigned weight 1/(рқ‘‘рқ‘Ј + 1), then the total
ГҚ
weight removed at each step is at most 1. Thus there must be at least рқ‘Ј 1/(рқ‘‘рқ‘Ј + 1)
steps.
Some probabilistic proofs, especially those involving linearity of expectations, can be
derandomized this way into an efficient deterministic algorithm. However, for many
other proofs (such as Ramsey lower bounds from Section 1.1), it is not known how to
derandomize the proof.

Proposition 2.4.2 (A cheap sampling bound)


Every tetrahedron-free 3-graph on рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 4 vertices has at most 34 рқ‘ӣ3 edges.
Proof. Let рқ‘Ҷ be a 4-vertex subset chosen uniformly at random. If the graph has


рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ3 edges, then the expected number of edges induced by рқ‘Ҷ is 4рқ‘қ by linearity of
expectations (why?).
Since the 3-graph is tetrahedron-free, рқ‘Ҷ induces at most 3 edges. Therefore, 4рқ‘қ вүӨ 3.


Thus the total number of edges is рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ3 вүӨ 34 рқ‘ӣ3 .
в–Ў
Why stop at sampling four vertices? Can we do better by sampling five vertices? To
run the above argument, we will know how many edges can there be in a 5-vertex
tetrahedron-free graph.
Lemma 2.4.3

A 5-vertex tetrahedron-free 3-graph has at most 7 edges.
Proof. We can convert a 5-vertex 3-graph рқҗ» to a 5-vertex graph рқҗә, by replacing each

triple by its complement. Then рқҗ» being tetrahedron-free is equivalent to рқҗә not having a
vertex of degree 4. The maximum number of edges in a 5-vertex graph with maximum
degree at most 3 is вҢҠ3 В· 5/2вҢӢ = 7 (check this can be achieved).
в–Ў

By sampling рқ‘  vertices and using brute-force search to solve the рқ‘ -vertex problem,
we can improve the upper bound by taking larger values of рқ‘ . In fact in principle, if
we had unlimited computational power, we can arbitrarily close to optimum by taking
sufficiently large рқ‘  (why?). However, this is not a practical method due to the cost of

2.5 Unbalancing lights
Consider an рқ‘ӣ Г— рқ‘ӣ array of light bulbs. Initially some arbitrary subset of the light bulbs
are turned on. We are allowed up toggle the lights (on/off) for an entire row or column
at a time. How many lights can be guarantee to turn on?
If we flip each row/column independently with probability 1/2, then on expectation,
we get exactly half of the lights to turn on. Can we do better?
In the probabilistic method, not every step has to be random. A better strategy is to
first flip all the columns randomly, and then decide what to do with each row greedily
based on what has happened so far. This is captured in the following theorem, where
the left-hand side represents
# {bulbs on} вҲ’ # {bulbs off} .

2.6 Crossing number inequality
Consider drawings of graphs on a plane using continuous curves as edges.
The crossing number cr(рқ‘®) is the minimum number of crossings in a drawing of рқҗә.
A graph is planar if cr(рқҗә) = 0.
The graphs рқҗҫ3,3 and рқҗҫ5 are non-planar. Furthermore, the following theorem characterizes these two graphs as the only obstructions to planarity:
KuratowskiвҖҷs theorem (1930). Every non-planar graph contains a subgraph that is
topologically homeomorphic to рқҗҫ3,3 or рқҗҫ5 .
WagnerвҖҷs theorem (1937). A graph is planar if and only if it does not have рқҗҫ3,3 or рқҗҫ5
as a minor.

25

2 Linearity of Expectations
(It is not too hard to show that WagnerвҖҷs theorem and KuratowskiвҖҷs theorem are
equivalent)
If a graph has a lot of edges, is it guaranteed to have a lot of crossings no matter how
it is drawn in the plane?
Question 2.6.1

What is the minimum possible number of crossings that a drawing of:
вҖў рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ ? (HillвҖҷs conjecture)
вҖў рқҗҫрқ‘ӣ,рқ‘ӣ ? (Zarankiewicz conjecture; TurГЎnвҖҷs brick factory problem)
вҖў a graph on рқ‘ӣ vertices and рқ‘ӣ2 /100 edges?
The following result, due to AjtaiвҖ“ChvГЎtalвҖ“NewbornвҖ“SzemerГ©di (1982) and Leighton
(1984), lower bounds the number of crossings for graphs with many edges.
Theorem 2.6.2 (Crossing number inequality)

In a graph рқҗә = (рқ‘ү, рқҗё), if |рқҗё | вүҘ 4|рқ‘ү |, then
cr(рқҗә) вүі

|рқҗё | 3
.
|рқ‘ү | 2

Remark 2.6.3. The constant 4 in |рқҗё | вүҘ 4 |рқ‘ү | can be replaced by any constant greater

than 3 (at the cost of changing the constant in the conclusion). On the other hand,
by considering a large triangular grid, we get a planar graph with average degree
arbitrarily close to 6.
Corollary 2.6.4

In a graph рқҗә = (рқ‘ү, рқҗё), if |рқҗё | вүі |рқ‘ү | 2 , then cr(рқҗә) вүі |рқ‘ү | 4 .
Proof. The proof has three steps, starting with some basic facts on planar graphs.

Step 1: From zero to one.
Recall EulerвҖҷs formula: рқ‘Ј вҲ’ рқ‘’ + рқ‘“ = 2 for every connected planar drawing of graph.
Here рқ‘Ј is the number of vertices, рқ‘’ the number of edges, and рқ‘“ the number of faces
(connected components of the complement of the drawing, including the outer infinite
region).
For every connected planar graph with at least one cycle, 3|рқҗ№ | вүӨ 2|рқҗё | since every face
is adjacent to вүҘ 3 edges, whereas every edge is adjacent to exactly 2 faces. Plugging
into EulerвҖҷs formula, |рқҗё | вүӨ 3|рқ‘ү | вҲ’ 6.

26

2.6 Crossing number inequality
Thus |рқҗё | вүӨ 3|рқ‘ү | for all planar graphs. Hence cr(рқҗә) > 0 whenever |рқҗё | > 3|рқ‘ү |.
Step 2: From one to many.
The above argument gives us one crossing. Next, we will use it to obtain many
crossings.
By deleting one edge for each crossing, we get a planar graph, so |рқҗё | вҲ’ cr(рқҗә) вүӨ 3|рқ‘ү |,
that is
cr(рқҗә) вүҘ |рқҗё | вҲ’ 3|рқ‘ү |.
This is a вҖңcheap bound.вҖқ For graphs with |рқҗё | = Оҳ(рқ‘ӣ2 ), this gives cr(рқҗә) вүі рқ‘ӣ2 . This is
not a great bound. We next will use the probabilistic method to boost this bound.
Step 3: Bootstrapping.
Let рқ‘қ вҲҲ [0, 1] to be decided. Let рқҗә вҖІ = (рқ‘ү вҖІ, рқҗё вҖІ) be obtained from рқҗә by randomly
keeping each vertex with probability рқ‘қ. Then
cr(рқҗә вҖІ) вүҘ |рқҗё вҖІ | вҲ’ 3|рқ‘ү вҖІ |.
So
E cr(рқҗә вҖІ) вүҘ E|рқҗё вҖІ | вҲ’ 3E|рқ‘ү вҖІ |
We have E cr(рқҗә вҖІ) вүӨ рқ‘қ 4 cr(рқҗә), E|рқҗё вҖІ | = рқ‘қ 2 |рқҗё | and E|рқ‘ү вҖІ | = рқ‘қE|рқ‘ү |. So
рқ‘қ 4 cr(рқҗә) вүҘ рқ‘қ 2 |рқҗё | вҲ’ 3рқ‘қ|рқ‘ү |.
Thus
cr(рқҗә) вүҘ рқ‘қ вҲ’2 |рқҗё | вҲ’ 3рқ‘қ вҲ’3 |рқ‘ү |.
Setting рқ‘қ = 4 |рқ‘ү | /|рқҗё | вҲҲ [0, 1] (here is where we use the hypothesis that |рқҗё | вүҘ 4 |рқ‘ү |)
so that 4рқ‘қ вҲ’3 |рқ‘ү | = рқ‘қ вҲ’2 |рқҗё |, we obtain cr(рқҗә) вүі |рқҗё | 3 /|рқ‘ү | 2 .
в–Ў
Remark 2.6.5. The above idea of boosting a cheap bound to a better bound is an

important one. We saw a version of this idea in Section 2.4 where we sampled a
constant number of vertices to deduce upper bounds on the hypergraph TurГЎn number. In the above crossing number inequality application, we are also applying some
preliminary cheap bound to some sampled induced subgraph, though this time the
sampled subgraph has super-constant size.
It is tempting to modify the proof by sampling edges instead of vertices, but this does
not work.

3.2 Heilbronn triangle problem
Question 3.2.1

How can one place рқ‘ӣ points in the unit square so that no three points forms a triangle
with small area?
Let
О”(рқ‘ӣ) = sup

min area( рқ‘қрқ‘һрқ‘ҹ)/

distinct

Naive constructions fair poorly. E.g., рқ‘ӣ points around a circle has a triangle of area
Оҳ(1/рқ‘ӣ3 ) (the triangle formed by three consectutive points has side lengths вүҚ 1/рқ‘ӣ and
angle рқңғ = (1 вҲ’ 1/рқ‘ӣ)2рқңӢ). Even worse is arranging points on a grid, as you would get
triangles of zero area.
Heilbronn conjectured that О”(рқ‘ӣ) = рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘ӣвҲ’2 ).
KomlГіs, Pintz, and SzemerГ©di (1982) disproved the conjecture, showing О”(рқ‘ӣ) вүі
рқ‘ӣвҲ’2 log рқ‘ӣ. They used an elaborate probabilistic construction. Here we show a much
simpler version probabilistic construction that gives a weaker bound О”(рқ‘ӣ) вүі рқ‘ӣвҲ’2 .
Remark 3.2.2 (Upper bounds). For a long time, the best upper bound known was

О”(рқ‘ӣ) вүӨ рқ‘ӣвҲ’8/7+рқ‘ң(1) due to KomlГіs, Pintz, and SzemerГ©di (1981). This was recently
improved to О”(рқ‘ӣ) вүӨ рқ‘ӣвҲ’8/7вҲ’рқ‘җ by Cohen, Pohoata, and Zakharov (2023+).
Theorem 3.2.3 (Many points without small area triangles)

For every positive integer рқ‘ӣ, there exists a set of рқ‘ӣ points in [0, 1] 2 such that every
triple spans a triangle of area вүҘ рқ‘җрқ‘ӣвҲ’2 , for some absolute constant рқ‘җ > 0.
Proof. Choose 2рқ‘ӣ points at random. For every three random points рқ‘қ, рқ‘һ, рқ‘ҹ, let us

and рқ‘Ҙ + О”рқ‘Ҙ, we find

3.4 High girth and high chromatic number
If a graph has a рқ‘ҳ-clique, then you know that its chromatic number is at least рқ‘ҳ.
Conversely, if a graph has high chromatic number, is it always possible to certify this
fact from some вҖңlocal informationвҖқ?
Surprisingly, the answer is no. The following ingenious construction shows that a
graph can be вҖңlocally tree-likeвҖқ while still having high chromatic number.
The girth of a graph is the length of its shortest cycle.

For all рқ‘ҳ, в„“, there exists a graph with girth > в„“ and chromatic number > рқ‘ҳ.

appearing with probability рқ‘қ independently).

3.5 Random greedy coloring
In Section 1.3, we saw a simple argument showing that every рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph
with than 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 edges is 2-colorable (meaning that we can color the vertices red/blue
without no monochromatic edge). Take a moment to remember the proof.
In this section, we improve this result. The next result gives the current best known
bound.
Theorem 3.5.1 (Radhakrishnan and Srinivasan (2000))

There
вҲҡпёғ is some constant рқ‘җ > 0 so that every рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph with at most
рқ‘җ logрқ‘ҳ рқ‘ҳ 2 рқ‘ҳ edges is 2-colorable.
Recall from Section 1.3 that there exists a non-2-colorable рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph on
рқ‘ҳ 2 vertices and рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘ҳ 2 2 рқ‘ҳ ) edges, via a random construction.
Here we present a simpler proof, based on a random greedy coloring, due to Cherkashin
and Kozik (2015), following an approach of PluhaГЎr (2009).
Proof. Consider a рқ‘ҳ-graph with рқ‘ҡ edges.

Let us order the vertices using a uniformly random chosen permutation.
Color vertices greedily from left to right: color a vertex blue unless it would create a
monochromatic edge, in which case color it red (i.e., every red vertex is the final vertex
in an edge with all earlier рқ‘ҳ вҲ’ 1 vertices have already been colored blue).

33

3 Alterations
The resulting coloring has no blue edges. The greedy coloring succeeds if it does not
create a red edge.
Analyzing a greedy coloring is tricky, since the color of a single vertex may depend on
the entire history. Instead, we identify a specific feature that necessarily results from
a unsuccessful coloring.
If there is a red edge, then there must be two edges рқ‘’, рқ‘“ so that the last vertex of рқ‘’
is the first vertex of рқ‘“ . Call such pair (рқ‘’, рқ‘“ ) conflicting (note that whether (рқ‘’, рқ‘“ ) is
conflicting depends on the random ordering of the vertices, but not on how we assigned
colors).
What is the probability of seeing a conflicting pair? Here is the randomness comes
from the random ordering of vertices.
Each pair of edges with exactly one vertex in common conflicts with probability

4.1 Does a typical random graph contain a triangle?
We begin with the following motivating question. Recall that the ErdЕ‘sвҖ“RГ©nyi random
graph рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) is the рқ‘ӣ-vertex graph with edge probability рқ‘қ.
Question 4.1.1

For which рқ‘қ = рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ does рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) contain a triangle with probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1)?
(We sometimes abbreviate вҖңwith probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1) by вҖңwith high probabilityвҖқ or
simply вҖңwhpвҖқ. In some literature, this is also called вҖңasymptotically almost surelyвҖқ or
вҖңa.a.s.вҖқ)
By computing Eрқ‘Ӣ (also known as the first moment), we deduce the following.
Proposition 4.1.2

If рқ‘ӣрқ‘қ вҶ’ 0, then рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) is triangle-free with probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1).
Proof. Let рқ‘Ӣ be the number of triangles in рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ). We know from linearity of

expectations that
 
рқ‘ӣ 3
Eрқ‘Ӣ =
рқ‘қ вүҚ рқ‘ӣ3 рқ‘қ 3 = рқ‘ң(1).
3
Thus, by MarkovвҖҷs inequality,
P(рқ‘Ӣ вүҘ 1) вүӨ Eрқ‘Ӣ = рқ‘ң(1).
In other words, рқ‘Ӣ = 0 with probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1).

в–Ў

In other words, when рқ‘қ вүӘ 1/рқ‘ӣ, рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) is triangle-free with high probaiblity (recall
that рқ‘қ вүӘ 1/рқ‘ӣ means рқ‘қ = рқ‘ң(1/рқ‘ӣ); see asymptotic notation guide at the beginning of
these notes).
What about when рқ‘қ вү« 1/рқ‘ӣ? Can we conclude that рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) contains a triangle with
high probability? In this case Eрқ‘Ӣ вҶ’ вҲһ, but this is not enough to conclude that

37

4 Second Moment
P(рқ‘Ӣ вүҘ 1) = 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1), since we have not ruled out the probability that рқ‘Ӣ is almost
always zero but extremely large with some tiny probability.
An important technique in probabilistic combinatorics is to show that some random
variable is concentrated around its mean. This would then imply that outliers are
unlikely.
We will see many methods in this course on proving concentrations of random variables. In this chapter, we begin with the simplest method. It is usually easiest to
execute and it requires not much hypotheses. The downside is that it only produces
relatively weak (though still useful enough) concentration bounds.
Second moment method: show that a random variable is concentrated near its mean
by bounding its variance.
Definition 4.1.3 (Variance)

The variance of a random variable рқ‘Ӣ is
Var[рқ‘Ӣ] := E[(рқ‘Ӣ вҲ’ Eрқ‘Ӣ) 2 ] = E[рқ‘Ӣ 2 ] вҲ’ E[рқ‘Ӣ] 2 .
The covariance of two random variables рқ‘Ӣ and рқ‘Ң (jointly distributed) is
Cov[рқ‘Ӣ, рқ‘Ң ] := E[(рқ‘Ӣ вҲ’ Eрқ‘Ӣ)(рқ‘Ң вҲ’ Eрқ‘Ң )] = E[рқ‘Ӣрқ‘Ң ] вҲ’ E[рқ‘Ӣ]E[рқ‘Ң ].
(Exercise: check the second equality in the definitions of variance and covariance
above).
Remark 4.1.4 (Notation convention). It is standard to use the Greek letter рқңҮ for the

mean, and рқңҺ 2 for the variance. Here рқңҺ вүҘ 0 is the standard deviation.
The following basic result provides a concentration bound based on the variance.
Theorem 4.1.5 (ChebyshevвҖҷs inequality)

Let рқ‘Ӣ be a random variable with mean рқңҮ and variance рқңҺ 2 . For any рқңҶ > 0
P(|рқ‘Ӣ вҲ’ рқңҮ| вүҘ рқңҶрқңҺ) вүӨ рқңҶвҲ’2 .
Proof. By MarkovвҖҷs inequality,

Remark 4.1.6. Concentration bounds that show small probability of deviating from

the mean are called tail bounds (more precisely: upper tail for рқ‘Ӣ вүҘ рқңҮ + рқ‘Һ and lower tail

Example 4.1.10 (Sum of independent Bernoulli). Suppose рқ‘Ӣ = рқ‘Ӣ1 + В· В· В· + рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ with

each рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– being an independent Bernoulli random variables with P(рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– = 1) = рқ‘қ and

Note that the above computation remains identical even if we only knew that the рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– вҖҷs
are pairwise uncorrelated (much weaker than assuming full independence).
Here the вҖңtail probabilityвҖқ (the bound hidden in вҖңwhpвҖқ) decays polynomially in the
deviation. Later on we will derive much sharper rates of decay (exponential) using
more powerful tools such as the Chernoff bound when the r.v.вҖҷs are independent.
Let us now return to the problem of determining when рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) contains a triangle
whp.
Theorem 4.1.11

If рқ‘ӣрқ‘қ вҶ’ вҲһ, then рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) contains a triangle with probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1).
Proof. Label the vertices by [рқ‘ӣ]. Let рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– рқ‘— be the indicator random variable of the edge

рқ‘– рқ‘—, so that рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– рқ‘— = 1 if the edge is present, and рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– рқ‘— = 0 if the edge is not present in the
random graph. Let us write

Then the number of triangles in рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) is given by

Here is what we have learned so far: for рқ‘қ = рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ and as рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ вҲһ,

if рқ‘ӣрқ‘қ вҶ’ вҲһ.

We say that 1/рқ‘ӣ is a threshold for containing a triangle, in the sense that if рқ‘қ grows
asymptotically faster than this threshold, i.e., рқ‘қ вү« 1/рқ‘ӣ, then the event occurs with
probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1), while if рқ‘қ вүӘ 1/рқ‘ӣ, then the event occurs with probability рқ‘ң(1).
Note that the definition of a threshold ignores leading constant factors (so that it is also
correct to say that 2/рқ‘ӣ is also a threshold for containing a triangle). Determining the
thresholds of various properties in random graphs (as well as other random settings)
is a central topic in probabilistic combinatorics. We will discuss thresholds in more
depth later in this chapter.
What else might you want to know about the probability that рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) contains a
triangle?
Remark 4.1.12 (Poisson limit). What if рқ‘ӣрқ‘қ вҶ’ рқ‘җ > 0 for some constant рқ‘җ > 0? It

turns out in this case that the number of triangles of рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) approaches a Poisson
distribution with constant mean. You will show this in the homework. It will be
done via the method of moments: if рқ‘Қ is some random variable with sufficiently
nice properties (known as вҖңdetermined by momentsвҖқ, which holds for many common
distributions such as the Poisson distribution and the normal distribution), and рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ is
a sequence of random variables such that Eрқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ вҶ’ Eрқ‘Қ рқ‘ҳ for all nonnegative integers рқ‘ҳ,
then рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ converges in distribution to рқ‘Қ.
Remark 4.1.13 (Asymptotic normality). Suppose рқ‘ӣрқ‘қ вҶ’ вҲһ. From the above proof,

we also deduce that рқ‘Ӣ вҲј Eрқ‘Ӣ, i.e., the number of triangles is concentrated around its
mean. In fact, we know much more. It turns out that the number рқ‘Ӣ of triangles in
рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, вҲҡ
рқ‘қ) is asymptotically normal, meaning that it satisfies a central limit theorem: (рқ‘Ӣ вҲ’
Eрқ‘Ӣ)/ Var рқ‘Ӣ converges in distribution to the standard normal рқ‘Ғ (0, 1) in distribution.
This was shown by RucinskiвҲҡ (1988) via the method of moments, by computing the
рқ‘ҳ-th moment of (рқ‘Ӣ вҲ’ Eрқ‘Ӣ)/ Var рқ‘Ӣ in the limit, and showing that it agrees with the
рқ‘ҳ-th moment of the standard normal.

4 Second Moment
In the homework, you will prove the asymptotic normality of рқ‘Ӣ using a later-found
method of projections. The idea is to show that that рқ‘Ӣ close to another random variable
that is already known to be asymptotically normal by checking that their difference
has negligible variance. For triangle counts, when рқ‘қ вү« рқ‘ӣвҲ’1/2 , we can compare the
number of triangles to the number of edges after a normalization. The method can be
further modified for greater generality. See the В§6.4 in the book Random Graphs by
Janson, ЕҒuczak, and Rucinski (2000).
Remark 4.1.14 (Better tail bounds). Later on we will use more powerful tools (in-

cluding martingale methods and Azuma-Hoeffding inequalities, and also Janson inequalities) to prove better tail bounds on triangle (and other subgraph) counts.

4.2 Thresholds for fixed subgraphs
In the last section, we determined the threshold for рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) to contain a triangle. What
about other subgraphs instead of a triangle? In this section, we give a complete answer
to this question for any fixed subgraph.
Question 4.2.1

What is the threshold for containing a fixed рқҗ» as a subgraph?
In other words, we wish to find some sequence рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ so that:
вҖў (0-statement) if рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ 0 (i.e., рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ вүӘ рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ ), then рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ ) contains рқҗ» with
probability рқ‘ң(1);
вҖў (1-statement) if рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ вҲһ (i.e., рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ вү« рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ ), then рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ ) contains рқҗ» with
probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1).
(It is not a priori clear why such a threshold exists in the first place. In fact, threshold
always exist for monotone properties, as we will see in the next section.)

the expectation. For the property of containing рқҗ», the example suggests that we should

4.3 Thresholds
Previously, we computed the threshold for containing a fixed рқҗ» as a subgraph. In
this section, we take a detour from the discussion of the second moment method and
discuss thresholds in more detail.
We begin by discussing the concept more abstractly by first defining the threshold of
any monotone property on subsets. Then we show that thresholds always exist.
Thresholds form a central topic in probabilistic combinatorics. For any given property,
it is natural to ask the following questions:
1. Where is the threshold?
2. Is the transition sharp? (And more precisely, what is width of the transition
window?)
We understand thresholds well for many basic graph properties, but for many others,
it can be a difficult problem. Also, one might think that one must first understand
the location of the threshold before determining the nature of the phase transition, but
surprisingly this is actually not always the case. There are powerful results that can
sometimes show a sharp threshold without identifying the location of the threshold.
Here is some general setup, before specializing to graphs.
Let О© be some finite set (ground set). Let О© рқ‘қ be a random subset of О© where each
element is included with probability рқ‘қ independently.
An increasing property, also called monotone property, on subsets of О© is some
binary property so that if рқҗҙ вҠҶ О© satisfies the property, any superset of рқҗҙ automatically
satisfies the property.
A property is trivial if all subsets of О© satisfy the property, or if all subsets of О© do not
satisfy the property. From now on, we only consider non-trivial monotone properties.

46

4.3 Thresholds
A graph property is a property that only depends on isomorphism classes of graphs.
Whether the random graph рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) satisfies a given property can be cast in our setup

by viewing рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) as О© рқ‘қ with О© = [рқ‘ӣ]
2 .
Here are some examples of increasing properties for subgraphs of a given set of
vertices:
вҖў Contains some given subgraph
вҖў Connected
вҖў Has perfect matching
вҖў Hamiltonian
вҖў non-3-colorable
A family F вҠҶ P (О©) of subsets of О© is called an up-set if whenever рқҗҙ вҲҲ F and рқҗҙ вҠҶ рқҗө,
then рқҗө вҲҲ F . Increasing property is the same as being an element of an up-set. We
will use these two terms interchangeably.
Definition 4.3.1 (Threshold)

Let О© = О© (рқ‘ӣ) be a finite set and F = F (рқ‘ӣ) an monotone property of subsets of О©. We
say that рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ is a threshold for F if,
(
P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ вҲҲ F ) вҶ’

0 if рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ 0,
1 if рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘һ рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ вҲһ.

Remark 4.3.2. The above definition is only for increasing properties. We can similarly

define the threshold for decreasing properties by an obvious modification. An example
of a non-monotone property is sontaining some рқҗ» as an induced subgraph. Some (but
not all) non-monotone properties also have thresholds, though we will not discuss it
here.
Remark 4.3.3. From the definition, we see that if рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ and рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣвҖІ are both thresholds of

the same property, then they must be within a constant factor of each other (exercise:
check this). Thus it makes sense to say вҖңthe thresholdвҖқ rather than вҖңa threshold.вҖқ

Existence of threshold
Question 4.3.4 (Existence of threshold)

Does every non-trivial monotone property have a threshold?

47

4 Second Moment
How would a monotone property not have a threshold? Perhaps one could have
P(О©1/рқ‘ӣ вҲҲ F ) and P(О© (log рқ‘ӣ)/рқ‘ӣ вҲҲ F ) вҲҲ [1/10, 9/10] for all sufficiently large рқ‘ӣ?
Before answer this question, let us consider an even more elementary claim.
Theorem 4.3.5 (Monotonicity of satisfying probability)

Let О© be a finite set and F a non-trivial monotone property of subsets of О©. Then
рқ‘қ вҶҰвҶ’ P(О© рқ‘қ вҲҲ F ) is a strictly increasing function of рқ‘қ вҲҲ [0, 1].
Let us give two related proofs of this basic fact. Both are quite instructive. Both are
based on coupling of random processes.
Proof 1. Let 0 вүӨ рқ‘қ < рқ‘һ вүӨ 1. Consider the following process to generate two random

subsets of О©. For each рқ‘Ҙ, generate uniform рқ‘Ўрқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ [0, 1] independently at random. Let
рқҗҙ = {рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ О© : рқ‘Ўрқ‘Ҙ вүӨ рқ‘қ}

and

рқҗө = {рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ О© : рқ‘Ўрқ‘Ҙ вүӨ рқ‘һ} .

Then рқҗҙ has the same distribution as О© рқ‘қ and рқҗө has the same distribution as О©рқ‘һ .
Furthermore, since рқ‘қ < рқ‘һ, we always have рқҗҙ вҠҶ рқҗө. Since F is monotone, рқҗҙ вҲҲ F
implies рқҗө вҲҲ F . Thus
P(О© рқ‘қ вҲҲ F ) = P( рқҗҙ вҲҲ F ) вүӨ P(рқҗө вҲҲ F ) = P(О©рқ‘һ вҲҲ F ).
To see that the inequality strict, we simply have to observe that with positive probability,
one has рқҗҙ вҲү F and рқҗө вҲҲ F (e.g., if all рқ‘Ў рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ ( рқ‘қ, рқ‘һ], then рқҗҙ = вҲ… and рқҗө = О©).
в–Ў
In the second proof, the idea is to reveal a random subset of О© in independent random
stages.
Proof 2. (By two-round exposure) Let 0 вүӨ рқ‘қ < рқ‘һ вүӨ 1. Note that рқҗө = О©рқ‘һ has the same

distribution as the union of two independent рқҗҙ = О© рқ‘қ and рқҗҙвҖІ = О© рқ‘қ вҖІ , where рқ‘қвҖІ is chosen
to satisfy 1 вҲ’ рқ‘һ = (1 вҲ’ рқ‘қ)(1 вҲ’ рқ‘қвҖІ) (check that the probability that each element occurs
is the same in the two processes). Thus
P( рқҗҙ вҲҲ F ) вүӨ P( рқҗҙ вҲӘ рқҗҙвҖІ вҲҲ F ) = P(рқҗө вҲҲ F ).
Like earlier, to observe that the inequality is strict, one observes that with positive
probability, one has рқҗҙ вҲү F and рқҗҙ вҲӘ рқҗҙвҖІ вҲҲ F .
в–Ў
The above technique (generalized from two round exposure to multiple round exposures) gives a nice proof of the following theorem (originally proved using the
KruskalвҖ“Katona theorem).1
1 (Thresholds for random subspaces of Fрқ‘ӣ ) The proof of the BollobГЎsвҖ“Thomason paper using the
рқ‘һ

4.3 Thresholds

Theorem 4.3.6 (Existence of thresholds: BollobГЎs and Thomason 1987)

Every sequence of nontrivial monotone properties has a threshold.
The theorem follows from the next non-asymptotic claim.
Lemma 4.3.7 (Multiple round exposure)

Let О© be a finite set and F some non-trivial monotone property. If рқ‘қ вҲҲ [0, 1] and рқ‘ҡ
is nonnegative integer. Then
P(О© рқ‘қ вҲү F ) вүӨ P(О© рқ‘қ/рқ‘ҡ вҲү F ) рқ‘ҡ .
Proof. Consider рқ‘ҡ independent copies of О© рқ‘қ/рқ‘ҡ , and let рқ‘Ң be their union. Since F is

monotone increasing, if рқ‘Ң вҲү F , then none of the рқ‘ҡ copies lie in F . Hence
P(рқ‘Ң вҲү F ) вүӨ P(О© рқ‘қ/рқ‘ҡ вҲү F ) рқ‘ҡ .
Note that рқ‘Ң has the same distribution as О©рқ‘һ for some рқ‘һ вүӨ рқ‘қ. So P(О© рқ‘қ вҲү F ) вүӨ
P(О©рқ‘һ вҲү F ) = P(рқ‘Ң вҲү F ) by Theorem 4.3.5. Combining the two inequalities gives the
result.
в–Ў
Proof of Theorem 4.3.6. Since рқ‘қ вҶҰвҶ’ P(О© рқ‘қ вҲҲ F ) is a continuous strictly increasing

function from 0 to 1 as рқ‘қ goes from 0 to 1 (in fact it is a polynomial in рқ‘қ), there is
some unique вҖңcritical probabilityвҖқ рқ‘қ рқ‘җ so that P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘җ вҲҲ F ) = 1/2.
It remains to check for every рқңҖ > 0, there is some рқ‘ҡ = рқ‘ҡ(рқңҖ) (not depending on the
property) so that
P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘җ /рқ‘ҡ вҲү F ) вүҘ 1 вҲ’ рқңҖ

and P(О©рқ‘ҡ рқ‘қ рқ‘җ вҲү F ) вүӨ рқңҖ.

KruskalвҖ“Katona theorem is still relevant. For example, there is an interesting analog of this concept
for properties of subspaces of Fрқ‘һрқ‘ӣ , i.e., random linear codes instead of random graphs. The analogue
of the BollobГЎsвҖ“Thomason theorem was proved by Rossman (2020) via the the KruskalвҖ“Katona
approach. The multiple round exposure proof does not seem to work in the random subspace setting,
as one cannot write a subspace as a union of independent copies of smaller subspaces.
As an aside, I disagree with the use of the term вҖңsharp thresholdвҖқ in RossmanвҖҷs paper for describing
all thresholds for subspacesвҖ”one really should be looking at the cardinality of the subspaces rather
than their dimensions. In a related work by Guruswami, Mosheiff, Resch, Silas, and Wootters
(2022), they determine thresholds for random linear codes for properties that seem to be analogous
to the property that a random graph contains a given fixed subgraph. Here again I disagree with
them calling it a вҖңsharp threshold.вҖқ It is much more like a coarse threshold once you parameterize
by the cardinality of the subspace, which gives you a much better analogy to the random graph
setting.
Thresholds for random linear codes seems to an interesting topic that has only recently been
studied. I think there is more to be done here.

4 Second Moment
(here we write О©рқ‘Ў = О© if рқ‘Ў > 1.) Indeed, applying Lemma 4.3.7 with рқ‘қ = рқ‘қ рқ‘җ , we have
P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘җ /рқ‘ҡ вҲү F ) вүҘ P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘җ вҲү F ) 1/рқ‘ҡ = 2вҲ’1/рқ‘ҡ вүҘ 1 вҲ’ рқңҖ

if рқ‘ҡ вүҘ (log 2)/рқңҖ.

if рқ‘ҡ вүҘ log2 (1/рқңҖ).

Thus рқ‘қ рқ‘җ is a threshold for F .

In this case, the threshold is determined by the expected number of triangles Оҳ(рқ‘ӣ3 рқ‘қ 3 ),

increases from 0 to 1 as a continuous function of рқ‘җ вҲҲ (0, вҲһ). So, in particular, as

is on the same order as the threshold. In this case, we call it a coarse transition.
Example 4.3.9 (Containing a subgraph). Theorem 4.2.10 tells us that the threshold

for containing a fixed subgraph рқҗ» is рқ‘ӣвҲ’1/рқ‘ҡ(рқҗ») . Here the threshold is not always
determined by the expected number of copies of рқҗ». Instead, we need to look at
the вҖңdensest subgraphвҖқ рқҗ» вҖІ вҠҶ рқҗ» with the largest edge-vertex ratio (i.e., equivalent to
largest average degree). The threshold is determined by whether the expected number
of copies of рқҗ» вҖІ goes to zero or infinity.
Similar to the triangle case, we have a coarse threshold.

The analysis can also be generalized to containing one of several fixed subgraphs
рқҗ»1 , . . . , рқҗ» рқ‘ҳ .
Remark 4.3.10 (Monotone graph properties are characterized by subgraph containment). Every monotone graph property can be characterized as containing some

element of H for some H that could depend on the vertex set рқ‘ӣ. For example, the
property of connectivity corresponds to taking H to be all spanning trees. More
generally, one can take H to be the set of all minimal graphs satisfying the property.
When elements of H are unbounded in size, the problem of thresholds become quite
interesting and sometimes difficult.
The original ErdЕ‘sвҖ“RГ©nyi (1959) paper on random graphs already studied several
thresholds, such as the next two examples.

precisely, when рқ‘җ рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ рқ‘җ, by comparing moments one can show that the number of
isolated vertices is asymptotically Poisson.
In this example, the threshold is at (log рқ‘ӣ)/рқ‘ӣ. As we see above, the transition window
is Оҳ(1/рқ‘ӣ), much narrower the magnitude of the threshold. In particular, the event
probability goes from рқ‘ң(1) to 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1) when рқ‘қ increases from (1 вҲ’ рқӣҝ)(log рқ‘ӣ)/рқ‘ӣ to
(1 + рқӣҝ) (log рқ‘ӣ)/рқ‘ӣ for any fixed рқӣҝ > 0. In this case, we say that the property has a sharp
threshold at (log рқ‘ӣ)/рқ‘ӣ (here the leading constant factor is relevant, unlike the earlier
example of a coarse threshold).

In fact, a much stronger statement is true, connecting the above two examples: consider
a process where one adds a random edges one at a time, then with probability 1 вҲ’ рқ‘ң(1),

51

4 Second Moment
the graph becomes connected as soon as there are no more isolated vertices. Such
stronger characterization is called a hitting time result.
A similar statement is true if we replace вҖңis connectedвҖқ by вҖңhas a perfect matchingвҖқ
(assuming рқ‘ӣ even).
Example 4.3.13 (Perfect matching in a random hypergraph: ShamirвҖҷs problem).

Let рқҗә (3) (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) be a random hypergraph on рқ‘ӣ vertices, where each triple of vertices
appears as an edge with probability рқ‘қ. Assume that рқ‘ӣ is divisible by 3. What is the
threshold for the existence of a perfect matching (i.e., a set of рқ‘ӣ/3 edges covering all
vertices)?
It is easy to check that the property of having no isolated vertices has a sharp threshold
at рқ‘қ = 2рқ‘ӣвҲ’2 log рқ‘ӣ. Is this also a threshold for having a perfect matching? So for smaller
рқ‘қ, one cannot have a perfect matching due to having an isolated vertex. What about
larger рқ‘қ? This turns out to be a difficult problem known as вҖңShamirвҖҷs problemвҖқ.
A difficult result by Johansson, Kahn, and Vu (2008) (this paper won a Fulkerson Prize)
showed that there is some constant рқҗ¶ > 0 so that if рқ‘қ вүҘ рқҗ¶рқ‘ӣвҲ’2 log рқ‘ӣ then рқҗә (3) (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ)
contains a perfect matching with high probability. They also solved the problem much
generally for рқҗ»-factors in random рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraphs.
Recent exciting breakthroughs on the KahnвҖ“Kalai conjecture (2007) by Frankston,
Kahn, Narayanan, and Park (2021) and Park and Pham (2024) provide new and much
shorter proofs of this threshold for ShamirвҖҷs problem.
Recently, Kahn (2022) proved a sharp threshold result, and actually an even stronger
hitting time version, of ShamirвҖҷs problem, showing that with high probability, one has
a perfect matching as soon as there are no isolated vertices.

Sharp transition
In some of the examples, the probability that рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘қ) satisfies the property changes
quickly and dramatically as рқ‘қ crosses the threshold (physical analogy: similar to how
the structure of water changes dramatically as the temperature drops below freezing).
For example, while for connectivity, while рқ‘қ = log рқ‘ӣ/рқ‘ӣ is a threshold, we see that
рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, 0.99 log рқ‘ӣ/рқ‘ӣ) is whp not connected and рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, 1.01 log рқ‘ӣ/рқ‘ӣ) is whp connected,
unlike the situation for containing a triangle earlier. We call this the sharp threshold
phenomenon.

Definition 4.3.14 (Sharp thresholds)

We say that рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ is a sharp threshold for some property F on subsets of О© if, for every
рқӣҝ > 0,
(
0 if рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ вүӨ 1 вҲ’ рқӣҝ,
P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ вҲҲ F ) вҶ’
1 if рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 1 + рқӣҝ.
On the other hand, if there is some fixed рқңҖ > 0 and 0 < рқ‘җ < рқҗ¶ so that P(О© рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ вҲҲ F ) вҲҲ
[рқңҖ, 1 вҲ’ рқңҖ] for whenever рқ‘җ вүӨ рқ‘қ рқ‘ӣ /рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ вүӨ рқҗ¶, then we say that рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ is a coarse threshold.
As in Figure 4.1, the sharp/coarseness of a thresholds is about how quickly P(О© рқ‘қ вҲҲ F )
transitions from рқңҖ to 1 вҲ’ рқңҖ as рқ‘қ increases. How wide is the transition window for рқ‘қ? By
the BollobГЎsвҖ“Thomason theorem (Theorem 4.3.6) on the existence of thresholds, this
transition window always has width рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ ). If the transition window has width Оҳ(рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ )
for some рқңҖ > 0, then we have a coarse threshold. On the other hand, if the transition
window has width рқ‘ң(рқ‘ҹ рқ‘ӣ ) for every рқңҖ > 0, then we have a sharp threshold.
From earlier examples, we saw coarse thresholds for the вҖңlocalвҖқ property of containing
some given subgraph, as well as sharp thresholds for вҖңglobalвҖқ properties such as
connectivity. It turns out that this is a general phenomenon.
FriedgutвҖҷs sharp threshold theorem (1999), a deep and important result, completely
characterizes when a threshold is coarse versus sharp. We will not state FriedgutвҖҷs
theorem precisely here since it is rather technical (and actually not always easy to
apply). Let us just give a flavor. Roughly speaking, the theorem says that:
All monotone graph properties with a coarse threshold may be approximated by a local property.
In other words, informally, if a monotone graph property P has a coarse threshold,
then there is finite list of graph рқҗә 1 , . . . , рқҗә рқ‘ҡ such that P is вҖңclose toвҖқ the property of
containing one of рқҗә 1 , . . . , рқҗә рқ‘ҡ as a subgraph.

53

4 Second Moment
We need вҖңclose toвҖқ since the property could be вҖңcontains a triangle and has at least
log рқ‘ӣ edgesвҖқ, which is not exactly local but it is basically the same as вҖңcontains a
triangle.вҖқ
There is some subtlety here since we can allow very different properties depending on
the value of рқ‘ӣ. E.g., P could be the set of all рқ‘ӣ-vertex graphs that contain a рқҗҫ3 if рқ‘ӣ is
odd and рқҗҫ4 if рқ‘ӣ is even. FriedgutвҖҷs theorem tells us that if there is a threshold, then
there is a partition N = N1 вҲӘ В· В· В· вҲӘ N рқ‘ҳ such that on each Nрқ‘– , P is approximately the
form described in the previous paragraph.
In the last section, we derived that the property of containing some fixed рқҗ» has
threshold рқ‘ӣвҲ’1/рқ‘ҡ(рқҗ») for some rational number рқ‘ҡ(рқҗ»). It follows as a corollary of
FriedgutвҖҷs theorem that every coarse threshold must have this form.
Corollary 4.3.15 (of FriedgutвҖҷs sharp threshold theorem)

Suppose рқ‘ҹ (рқ‘ӣ) is a coarse threshold of some graph property. Then there is a partition
of N = N1 вҲӘ В· В· В· вҲӘ N рқ‘ҳ and rationals рқӣј1 , . . . , рқӣј рқ‘ҳ > 0 such that рқ‘ҹ (рқ‘ӣ) вүҚ рқ‘ӣвҲ’рқӣј рқ‘— for every
рқ‘ӣ вҲҲ Nрқ‘—.
In particular, if (log рқ‘ӣ)/рқ‘ӣ is a threshold of some monotone graph property (e.g., this is
the case for connectivity), then we automatically know that it must be a sharp threshold,
even without knowing anything else about the property. Likewise if the threshold has
the form рқ‘ӣвҲ’рқӣј for some irrational рқӣј.
The exact statement of FriedgutвҖҷs theorem is more cumbersome. We refer those who
are interested to FriedgutвҖҷs original 1999 paper and his later survey for details and
applications. This topic is connected more generally to an area known as the analysis
of boolean functions.
Also, it is known that the transition window of every monotone graph property is
(log рқ‘ӣ) вҲ’2+рқ‘ң(1) (FriedgutвҖ“вҖ“Kalai (1996), BourgainвҖ“Kalai (1997)).
Curiously, tools such as FriedgutвҖҷs theorem sometimes allow us to prove the existence
of a sharp threshold without being able to identify its exact location. For example, it is
an important open problem to understand where exactly is the transition for a random
graph to be рқ‘ҳ-colorable.
Conjecture 4.3.16 ( рқ‘ҳ -colorability threshold)

For every рқ‘ҳ вүҘ 3 there is some real constant рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ > 0 such that for any constant рқ‘‘ > 0,
(
P(рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘‘/рқ‘ӣ) is рқ‘ҳ-colorable) вҶ’

1

if рқ‘‘ < рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ ,

0

if рқ‘‘ > рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ .

We do know that there exists a sharp threshold for рқ‘ҳ-colorability.

1 if рқ‘‘ (рқ‘ӣ) < рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ (рқ‘ӣ) вҲ’ рқңҖ,
0 if рқ‘‘ (рқ‘ӣ) > рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ (рқ‘ӣ) + рқңҖ.

On the other hand, it is not known whether limрқ‘ӣвҶ’вҲһ рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ (рқ‘ӣ) exists, which would imply
Conjecture 4.3.16. Further bounds on рқ‘‘ рқ‘ҳ (рқ‘ӣ) are known, e.g. the landmark paper of
Achlioptas and Naor (2006) showing that for each fixed рқ‘‘ > 0, whp рқң’(рқҗә (рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘‘/рқ‘ӣ) вҲҲ
{рқ‘ҳ рқ‘‘ , рқ‘ҳ рқ‘‘ + 1} where рқ‘ҳ рқ‘‘ = min{рқ‘ҳ вҲҲ N : 2рқ‘ҳ log рқ‘ҳ > рқ‘‘}. Also see the later work of
Coja-Oghlan and Vilenchik (2013).

ГҚ
The main idea from the above proof is that the number of prime divisors рқ‘Ӣ = рқ‘қ рқ‘Ӣ рқ‘қ
(from the previous proof) behaves like a sum of independent random variables.
A sum of independent random variables often satisfy a central limit theorem (i.e.,
asymptotic normality, convergence to Gaussian), assuming some mild regularity hypotheses. In particular, we have the following corollary of the LindenbergвҖ“Feller
central limit theorem (see Durrett, Theorem 3.4.10):
Theorem 4.5.2 (Central limit theorem for sums of independent Bernoullis)

If рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ is a sum of independent
Bernoulli random variables, and Var рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ вҶ’ вҲһ as рқ‘ӣ вҶ’ вҲһ,
вҲҡ
then (рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ вҲ’ Eрқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ )/ Var рқ‘Ӣ converges to the normal distribution.
(Note that the divergent variance hypothesis is necessary and sufficient.)
In the setting of prime divisibility, we do not have genuine independence. Nevertheless,
it is natural to expect that рқңҲ(рқ‘Ҙ) still satisfies a central limit theorem. This is indeed the
case, and can be proved by comparing moments against a genuine sum of independent
random Bernoulli random variables.
Theorem 4.5.3 (Asymptotic normality: ErdЕ‘s and Kac 1940)

With рқ‘Ҙ вҲҲ [рқ‘ӣ] uniformly chosen at random, рқңҲ(рқ‘Ҙ) is asymptotically normal, i.e., for
every рқңҶ вҲҲ R,

The original proof of ErdЕ‘s and Kac verifies the above intuition using some more
involved results in analytic number theory. Simpler proofs have been subsequently
given, and we outline one such proof below, which is based on computing the moments

59

4 Second Moment
of the distribution. The idea of computing moments for this problem was first used by
Delange (1953), who was apparently not aware of the ErdЕ‘sвҖ“Kacs paper. Also see a
more modern account by Granville and Soundararajan (2007).
The following tool from probability theory allows us to verify asymptotic normality
from convergence of moments.
Theorem 4.5.4 (Method of moments)

Let рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ be a sequence of real valued random variables such that for every positive integer
рқ‘ҳ, limрқ‘ӣвҶ’вҲһ E[рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣрқ‘ҳ ] equals to the рқ‘ҳ-th moment of the standard normal distribution. Then
рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ converges in distribution to the standard normal, i.e., limрқ‘ӣвҶ’вҲһ P(рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ вүӨ рқ‘Һ) = P(рқ‘Қ вүӨ
рқ‘Һ) for every рқ‘Һ вҲҲ R, where рқ‘Қ is a standard normal.
Remark 4.5.5. The same conclusion holds for any probability distribution that is

вҖңdetermined by its moments,вҖқ i.e., there are no other distributions sharing the same
moments. Many common distributions that arise in practice, e.g., the Poisson distribution, satisfy this property. There are various sufficient conditions for guaranteeing
this moments property, e.g., CarlemanвҖҷs condition tells us that any probability distribution whose moments do not increase too quickly is determined by its moments. (See
Durrett В§3.3.5).
Proof of ErdЕ‘sвҖ“Kacs Theorem 4.5.3. We compare higher moments of рқ‘Ӣ = рқңҲ(рқ‘Ҙ) with

that of an idealized рқ‘Ң treating the prime divisors as truly random variables.

рқ‘Ј(рқ‘Ҙ).

4.6 Distinct sums
What is the largest subset of [рқ‘ӣ] all of whose subsets have distinct sums? Equivalently:
Question 4.6.1

For each рқ‘ҳ, what is the smallest рқ‘ӣ so that there exists рқ‘Ҷ вҠҶ [рқ‘ӣ] with |рқ‘Ҷ| = рқ‘ҳ and all 2 рқ‘ҳ
subset sums of рқ‘Ҷ are distinct?
E.g., рқ‘Ҷ = {1, 2, 22 , . . . , 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 } (the greedy choice).
We begin with an easy pigeonhole argument. Since all 2 рқ‘ҳ sums are distinct and are at
most рқ‘ҳрқ‘ӣ, we have 2 рқ‘ҳ вүӨ рқ‘ҳрқ‘ӣ. Thus рқ‘ӣ вүҘ 2 рқ‘ҳ /рқ‘ҳ.
ErdЕ‘s offered $300 for a proof or disproof of the following. It remains open.
Conjecture 4.6.2 (ErdЕ‘s)

рқ‘ӣ вүі 2рқ‘ҳ
Let us use the second moment to give a modest improvement on the earlier pigeonhole
argument. The main idea here is that, by second moment, most of the subset sums lie
within an рқ‘Ӯ (рқңҺ)-interval, so that we can improve on the pigeonhole estimate ignoring
outlier subset sums.

4.7 Weierstrass approximation theorem
We finish off the chapter with an application to analysis.
The Weierstrass approximation theorem says that every continuous real function on an
interval can be uniformly approximated by a polynomial.

4 Second Moment

67

5 Chernoff Bound
The Chernoff bound is an extremely useful bound on the tails of a sum of independent
random variables. It is proved by bounding the moment generating function. This
proof technique is interesting and important in its own right. We will see this proof
method come up again later on when we prove martingale concentration inequalities.
The method allows us to adapt the proof of the Chernoff bound to other distributions.
Let us give the proof in the most basic case for simplicity and clarity.
Theorem 5.0.1 (Chernoff bound)

Let рқ‘Ҷрқ‘ӣ = рқ‘Ӣ1 + В· В· В· + рқ‘Ӣрқ‘ӣ where рқ‘Ӣрқ‘– вҲҲ {вҲ’1, 1} uniformly iid. Let рқңҶ > 0. Then

вҲҡ

other hand, ChebyshevвҖҷs inequality is application in wider settings as it only requires
pairwise independence (for the second moment) as opposed to full independence.

Remark 5.0.2. The technique of considering the moment generating function can

be thought morally as taking an appropriately high moment.

The second moment method (Chebyshev + Markov) can be thought of as the first
iteration of this idea. By taking fourth moments (now requiring 4-wise independence
of the summands), we can obtain tail bounds of the form вүІ рқңҶвҲ’4 . And similarly with
higher moments.
In some applications, where one cannot assume independence, but can estimate some
high moments, the above philosophy can allow us to prove good tail bounds as well.

Remark 5.0.8. The bounds for upper and lower tails are necessarily asymmetric when

the probabilities are small. Why? Think about what happens when рқ‘Ӣ вҲј Bin(рқ‘ӣ, рқ‘җ/рқ‘ӣ),

one might naively predict by an incorrect application of the Chernoff bound formula.

5.1 Discrepancy
Given a hypergraph (i.e., set family), can we color the vertices red/blue so that every
edge has roughly the same number of red versus blue vertices? (Contrast this problem
to 2-coloring hypergraphs from Section 1.3.)

5 Chernoff Bound

Theorem 5.1.1

Let F be a collection of рқ‘ҡ subsets of [рқ‘ӣ]. Then вҲҡпёҒthere exists some assignment
[рқ‘ӣ] вҶ’ {вҲ’1, 1} so that the sum on every set in F is рқ‘Ӯ ( рқ‘ӣ log рқ‘ҡ) in absolute value.
Proof. Put Вұ1 iid uniformly at random on each vertex. On each edge, the probability

that the sum exceeds 2 рқ‘ӣ log рқ‘ҡ in absolute value is, by Chernoff bound, less than
2рқ‘’ вҲ’2 log рқ‘ҡ = 2/рқ‘ҡ 2 . By union bound over all
вҲҡпёҒ рқ‘ҡ edges, with probability greater than
1 вҲ’ 2/рқ‘ҡ вүҘ 0, no edge has sum exceeding 2 рқ‘ӣ log рқ‘ҡ.
в–Ў

Remark 5.1.4. More generally, Spencer proves that the same holds if vertices have

The idea, very roughly speaking, is to first generalize from {вҲ’1, 1}-valued assignments to [вҲ’1, 1]-valued assignments. Then the all-zero vector is a trivially satisfying
assignment. We then randomly, in iterations, alter the values from 0 to other values
вҲҡ
in [вҲ’1, 1], while avoiding potential violations (e.g., edges with sum close to 6 рқ‘ӣ in
absolute value), and finalizing a color of a color when its value moves to either вҲ’1 and
1.
SpencerвҖҷs original proof was not algorithmic, and he suspected that it could not be
made efficiently algorithmic. In a breakthrough result, Bansal (2010) gave an efficient
algorithm for producing a coloring with small discrepancy. Lovett and Meka (2015)
provided another element algorithm with a beautiful proof.

Remark 5.2.3 (Equiangular lines with a fixed angle). Given a fixed angle рқңғ, for large

рқ‘ӣ, how many lines in Rрқ‘ӣ through the origin can one place whose pairwise angles are all

is the same as asking for a set of unit vectors in Rрқ‘ӣ whose pairwise inner products are
Вұрқӣј. It turns out that for fixed рқӣј, the maximum number of lines grows linearly with the
dimension рқ‘ӣ, and the rate of growth depends on properties of рқӣј in relation to spectral
graph theory. We refer to the cited paper for details.

5.3 HajГіs conjecture counterexample
We begin by reviewing some classic result from graph theory. Recall some definitions:
вҖў рқҗ» is an induced subgraph of рқҗә if рқҗ» can be obtained from рқҗә by removing
vertices;
вҖў рқҗ» is a subgraph if рқҗә if рқҗ» can be obtained from рқҗә by removing vertices and
edges;
вҖў рқҗ» is a subdivision of рқҗә if рқҗ» can be obtained from a subgraph of рқҗә by contracting
induced paths to edges;
вҖў рқҗ» is a minor of рқҗә if рқҗ» can be obtained from a subgraph of рқҗә by by contracting
edges to vertices.
KuratowskiвҖҷs theorem (1930). Every graph without рқҗҫ3,3 and рқҗҫ5 as subdivisions as
subdivision is planar.
WagnerвҖҷs theorem (1937). Every graph free of рқҗҫ3,3 and рқҗҫ5 as minors is planar.
(There is a short argument shows that Kuratowski and WagnerвҖҷs theorems are equivalent.)
Four color theorem (Appel and Haken 1977) Every planar graph is 4-colorable.
Corollary: Every graph without рқҗҫ3,3 and рқҗҫ5 as minors is 4-colorable.

75

5 Chernoff Bound
The condition on рқҗҫ5 is clearly necessary, but what about рқҗҫ3,3 ? What is the вҖңrealвҖқ
reason for 4-colorability?
HadwidgerвҖҷs conjecture, below, remains a major conjectures in graph theory.
Conjecture 5.3.1 (Hadwiger 1936)

For every рқ‘Ў вүҘ 1, every graph without a рқҗҫрқ‘Ў+1 minor is рқ‘Ў-colorable.
вҖў рқ‘Ў = 1 trivial
вҖў рқ‘Ў = 2 nearly trivial (if рқҗә is рқҗҫ3 -minor-free, then itвҖҷs a tree)
вҖў рқ‘Ў = 3 elementary graph theoretic arguments
вҖў рқ‘Ў = 4 is equivalent to the 4-color theorem (Wagner 1937)
вҖў рқ‘Ў = 5 is equivalent to the 4-color theorem (RobertsonвҖ“SeymourвҖ“Thomas 1994;
this work won a Fulkerson Prize)
вҖў рқ‘Ў вүҘ 6 remains open
Let us explore a variation of HadwigerвҖҷs conjecture:
HajГіs conjecture. (1961) Every graph without a рқҗҫрқ‘Ў+1 -subdivision is рқ‘Ў-colorable.
HajГіs conjecture is true for рқ‘Ў вүӨ 3. However, it turns out to be false in general. Catlin
(1979) constructed counterexamples for all рқ‘Ў вүҘ 6 (рқ‘Ў = 4, 5 are still open).
It turns out that HajГіs conjecture is not just false, but very false.
ErdЕ‘sвҖ“Fajtlowicz (1981) showed that almost every graph is a counterexample (itвҖҷs a
good idea to check for potential counterexamples among random graphs!)
Theorem 5.3.2

Proof. If рқҗә had a рқҗҫрқ‘Ў -subdivision, say with рқ‘Ҷ вҠҶ рқ‘ү, |рқ‘Ҷ| = рқ‘Ў. Each pair of vertices of рқ‘Ҷ

are connected via a path, whose intermediate vertices are outside рқ‘Ҷ, and distinct for
different pairs of vertices.

Remark 5.3.3 (Quantitative question). One can ask the following quantitative ques-

tion regarding HadwidgerвҖҷs conjecture:
Can every graph without a рқҗҫрқ‘Ў+1 -minor can be properly colored with a small number of
colors?
Wagner (1964) showed that every graph without рқҗҫрқ‘Ў+1 -minor is 2рқ‘ЎвҲ’1 colorable.
Here is the proof: assume that the graph is connected. Take a vertex рқ‘Ј and let рқҗҝ рқ‘– be
the set of vertices with distance exactly рқ‘– from рқ‘Ј. The subgraph induced on рқҗҝ рқ‘– has no
рқҗҫрқ‘Ў -minor, since otherwise such a рқҗҫрқ‘Ў -minor would extend to a рқҗҫрқ‘Ў+1 -minor with рқ‘Ј. Then
by induction рқҗҝ рқ‘– is 2рқ‘ЎвҲ’2 -colorable (check base cases), and using alternating colors for
even and odd layers рқҗҝ рқ‘– yields a proper coloring of рқҗә.
This bound has been improved over time. Delcourt and Postle (2021+) showed that
every graph with no рқҗҫрқ‘Ў -minor is рқ‘Ӯ (рқ‘Ў log log рқ‘Ў)-colorable.
For more on HadwigerвҖҷs conjecture, see SeymourвҖҷs survey (2016).

6 LovГЎsz Local Lemma
The LovГЎsz local lemma (LLL) was introduced in the paper of ErdЕ‘s and LovГЎsz
(1975). It is a powerful tool in the probabilistic method.
In many problems, we wish to avoid a certain set of вҖңbad events.вҖқ Here are two easy
to handle scenarios:
вҖў (Complete independence) All the bad events are independent and have probability less than 1.
вҖў (Union bound) The sum of the bad event probabilities is less than 1.
The local lemma deals with an intermediate situation where there is a small amount of
local dependencies.
We saw an application of the LovГЎsz local lemma back in Section 1.1, where we used
it to lower bound Ramsey numbers. This chapter explores the local lemma and its
applications in depth.

6.1 Statement and proof
Definition 6.1.1 (Independence from a set of events)

Here we say that an event рқҗҙ0 is independent from events рқҗҙ1 , . . . , рқҗҙрқ‘ҡ if рқҗҙ0 is independent of every event of the form рқҗө1 вҲ§ В· В· В· вҲ§ рқҗөрқ‘ҡ (we sometimes omit the вҖңlogical andвҖқ
symbol вҲ§) where each рқҗөрқ‘– is either рқҗҙрқ‘– or рқҗҙрқ‘– , i.e.,
P( рқҗҙ0 рқҗө1 В· В· В· рқҗөрқ‘ҡ ) = P( рқҗҙ0 )P(рқҗө1 В· В· В· рқҗөрқ‘ҡ ),
or, equivalently, using BayesвҖҷs rule:
P( рқҗҙ0 |рқҗө1 В· В· В· рқҗөрқ‘ҡ ) = P( рқҗҙ0 ).
Given a collection of events, we can associate to it a dependency graph. This is a
slightly subtle notion, as we will explain. Technically speaking, the graph can be a
directed graph (=digraph), but for most applications, it will be sufficient (and easier)
to use undirected graphs.

79

6 LovГЎsz Local Lemma

Definition 6.1.2 (Dependency (di)graph)

Let рқҗҙ1 , . . . , рқҗҙрқ‘ӣ be events (the вҖңbad eventsвҖқ we wish to avoid). Let рқҗә be a (directed)
graph with vertex set [рқ‘ӣ]. We say that рқҗә is a dependency (di)graph for the events
рқҗҙ1 , . . . , рқҗҙрқ‘ӣ if, for for every рқ‘–, рқҗҙрқ‘– is independent from all {рқҗҙ рқ‘— : рқ‘— вҲү рқ‘Ғ (рқ‘–) вҲӘ {рқ‘–}} (рқ‘Ғ (рқ‘–)
is the set of (out)neighbors of рқ‘– in рқҗә).
Remark 6.1.3 (Non-uniqueness). Given a collection of events, there can be more

than one valid dependency graphs. For example, the complete graph is always a valid
dependency graph.
Remark 6.1.4 (Important!). Independence вү  pairwise independence

The dependency graph is not made by joining рқ‘– вҲј рқ‘— whenever рқҗҙрқ‘– and рқҗҙ рқ‘— are not
independent (i.e., P( рқҗҙрқ‘– рқҗҙ рқ‘— ) вү  P( рқҗҙрқ‘– )P( рқҗҙ рқ‘— )).
Example: suppose one picks рқ‘Ҙ1 , рқ‘Ҙ2 , рқ‘Ҙ3 вҲҲ Z/2Z uniformly and independently at random
and set, for each рқ‘– = 1, 2, 3 (indices taken mod 3), рқҗҙрқ‘– the event that рқ‘Ҙрқ‘–+1 + рқ‘Ҙрқ‘–+2 = 0.
Then these events are pairwise independent but not independent. So the empty graph
on three vertices is not a valid dependency graph (on the other hand, having at least
two edges makes it a valid dependency graph).
In practice, it is not too hard to construct a valid dependency graph, since most
applications of the LovГЎsz local lemma use the following setup (which we saw in
Section 1.1).
Setup 6.1.5 (Random variable model / hypergraph coloring)

Let {рқ‘Ҙрқ‘– : рқ‘– вҲҲ рқҗј} be a collection of independent random variables. Let рқҗё 1 , . . . , рқҗё рқ‘ӣ be
events where each рқҗёрқ‘– depends only on the variables indexed by some subset рқҗөрқ‘– вҠҶ рқҗј of
variables. A canonical dependency graph for the events рқҗё 1 , . . . , рқҗё рқ‘ӣ has vertex set [рқ‘ӣ]
and an edge рқ‘– рқ‘— whenever рқҗөрқ‘– вҲ© рқҗө рқ‘— вү  вҲ….
It is easy to check that the canonical dependency graph above is indeed a valid dependency graph.
Example 6.1.6 (Boolean satisfiability problem (SAT)). Given a CNF formula (con-

junctive normal form, i.e., and-of-orвҖҷs), e.g., (вҲ§ = and; вҲЁ = or)
(рқ‘Ҙ 1 вҲЁ рқ‘Ҙ 2 вҲЁ рқ‘Ҙ3 ) вҲ§ (рқ‘Ҙ 1 вҲЁ рқ‘Ҙ 2 вҲЁ рқ‘Ҙ 4 ) вҲ§ (рқ‘Ҙ 2 вҲЁ рқ‘Ҙ 4 вҲЁ рқ‘Ҙ 5 ) вҲ§ В· В· В·
the problem is to find a satisfying assignment with boolean variables рқ‘Ҙ 1 , рқ‘Ҙ2 , . . . . Many
problems in computer science can be modeled using this way. This problem can be
viewed as in Setup 6.1.5, where рқҗҙрқ‘– is the event that the рқ‘–-th clause is violated.

6.1 Statement and proof
The following formulation of the local lemma is easiest to apply and is the most
commonly used. It applies to settings where the dependency graph has small maximum
degree.

from a set of all other рқҗҙ рқ‘— except for at most рқ‘‘ of them. If
рқ‘’ рқ‘қ(рқ‘‘ + 1) вүӨ 1,

6.2 Coloring hypergraphs
Previously, in Theorem 1.3.1, we saw that every рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph with fewer
than 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 edges is 2-colorable. The next theorem gives a sufficient local condition for
2-colorability.
Theorem 6.2.1

A рқ‘ҳ-uniform hypergraph is 2-colorable if every edge intersects at most рқ‘’ вҲ’1 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 вҲ’ 1
other edges
Proof. For each edge рқ‘“ , let рқҗҙ рқ‘“ be the event that рқ‘“ is monochromatic. Then P( рқҗҙ рқ‘“ ) =

рқ‘қ := 2вҲ’рқ‘ҳ+1 . Each рқҗҙ рқ‘“ is independent from all рқҗҙ рқ‘“ вҖІ where рқ‘“ вҖІ is disjoint from рқ‘“ . Since
at most рқ‘‘ := рқ‘’ вҲ’1 2 рқ‘ҳвҲ’1 вҲ’ 1 edges intersect every edge, and рқ‘’(рқ‘‘ + 1) рқ‘қ вүӨ 1, so the local
lemma implies that with positive probability, none of the events рқҗҙ рқ‘“ occur.
в–Ў
Corollary 6.2.2

For рқ‘ҳ вүҘ 9, every рқ‘ҳ-uniform рқ‘ҳ-regular hypergraph is 2-colorable.
(Here рқ‘ҳ-regular means that every vertex lies in exactly рқ‘ҳ edges.)
Proof. Every edge intersects вүӨ рқ‘‘ = рқ‘ҳ (рқ‘ҳ вҲ’1) other edges. And рқ‘’(рқ‘ҳ (рқ‘ҳ вҲ’1) +1)2вҲ’рқ‘ҳ+1 < 1

for рқ‘ҳ вүҘ 9.
